The problem of the redn. of perchlorate ions is discussed in the light of recent results bearing in mind the perchlorate environmental contamination challenge and recent problems concerning the electrochem. stability of ClO4- ions.  The catalytic and electrocatalytic transformations are compared in the case of WC and Pt electrodes (catalysts).  Various methods for the detection of the occurrence of the redn. process are presented (radiotracer technique, potential step method and voltammetry).  The interpretation of the results of studies of corrosion processes of Al, Zn, Cu, Ni and Fe carried out in the presence of ClO4- ions cannot be considered as adequate without taking into consideration the redn. of ClO4- ions and all previous work should be revisited from the new findings.  Some elements of the mechanistic aspects of the complicated redn. processes are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5PErM-fqt_7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljQ5pvbnGsgmQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3sXmvF2mtbc%253D&amp;md5=c4a8e5e1f0d01521d0636fb7c6e3dd32</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1016/0022-0728(85)80090-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Colom, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">González-Tejera, M. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reduction of Perchlorate Ion on Ruthenium Electrodes in Aqueous Solutions</span>. <i>J. Electroanal. Chem. Interfacial Electrochem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1985</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>190</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">243</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">255</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/0022-0728(85)80090-5</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D190%26publication_year%3D1985%26pages%3D243-255%26journal%3DJ.%2BElectroanal.%2BChem.%2BInterfacial%2BElectrochem.%26author%3DF.%2BColom%26author%3DM.%2BJ.%2BGonz%25C3%25A1lez-Tejera%26title%3DReduction%2Bof%2BPerchlorate%2BIon%2Bon%2BRuthenium%2BElectrodes%2Bin%2BAqueous%2BSolutions%26doi%3D10.1016%252F0022-0728%252885%252980090-5&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b03279&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2F0022-0728%2885%2980090-5&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reduction of perchlorate ion on ruthenium electrodes in aqueous solutions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Colom, F.; Gonzalez-Tejera, M. J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Electroanalytical Chemistry and Interfacial Electrochemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1985</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">190</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1-2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">243-55</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JEIEBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0022-0728</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">During slow potential scanning (&lt;10 mV/s) of the H region at electrodeposited Ru electrodes (Ru/Pt) in HClO4 solns. at room temp., a rapid change from anodic to cathodic current is recorded following the peak of oxidn. of adsorbed H.  The max. of this current reversal appears at potentials around 120 mV (vs. std. H electrode).  The neg. sweep of the voltammogram shows a current peak that corresponds to redn. of adsorbed O.  Addn. of H2SO4 or HCl to HClO4 solns. proves that the current reversal is due to the presence of ClO4-.  Anal. of the soln. after arresting the pos. sweep in the potential region of current reversal shows the formation of Cl-.  The redn. of ClO4- on Ru electrodes appears to be a very slow process which is only detected at low potential scan rates but is markedly enhanced by temp. and ClO4- concn.  The results suggest that ClO4- redn. proceeds through interaction of the anion and H adsorbed on the Ru electrode to the final products of Cl- and H2O.  The process seems to be related to the electrocatalytic activity of the electrode surface and the noble metal.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoFRDCvghQT47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljQ5pvbnGsgmQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL2MXltFaisr8%253D&amp;md5=930e7e356dd4b954d0940b2e10865ef7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1016/j.tetlet.2014.12.145"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Unzner, T. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Magauer, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon–Fluorine Bond Activation for the Synthesis of Functionalized Molecules</span>. <i>Tetrahedron Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>56</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">877</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">883</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.tetlet.2014.12.145</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D56%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D877-883%26journal%3DTetrahedron%2BLett.%26author%3DT.%2BA.%2BUnzner%26author%3DT.%2BMagauer%26title%3DCarbon%25E2%2580%2593Fluorine%2BBond%2BActivation%2Bfor%2Bthe%2BSynthesis%2Bof%2BFunctionalized%2BMolecules%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.tetlet.2014.12.145&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b03279&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.tetlet.2014.12.145&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon-fluorine bond activation for the synthesis of functionalized molecules</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Unzner, Teresa A.; Magauer, Thomas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Tetrahedron Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">56</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">877-883</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">TELEAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0040-4039</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this review, we highlight recent progress in the activation of allylic and benzylic C-F bonds for the synthesis of functionalized mols.  The synthesis of fluorinated compds. as well as the use of these products as building blocks for the prepn. of complex mols. are fast growing research areas.  SN2' reactions of allylic difluoro and trifluoro compds. as well as transition-metal-catalyzed (nickel, palladium, platinum and copper) processes are described.  The C-F bond activation of 3-fluoropropenes was achieved with platinum- or organocatalysis.  Benzylic C-F bond activation was realized with magnesium by deprotonation leading to the formation of reactive quinone methide intermediates and with hydrogen bond donors by forming strong F···H interactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpQIOGxNd0KbLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljQ5pvbnGsgmQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXptFGgtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=d48469e7aca69dc19cb5addd1adc884c</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da90e1ae43b325-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lixLGvo99mbAg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1039/c3ee40847g"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Room-Temperature Stationary Sodium-Ion Batteries for Large-Scale Electric Energy Storage</span>. <i>Energy Environ. Room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries have attracted great attention particularly in large-scale elec. energy storage applications for renewable energy and smart grid because of the huge abundant sodium resources and low cost.  In this article, a variety of electrode materials including cathodes and anodes as well as electrolytes for room-temp. stationary sodium-ion batteries are briefly reviewed.  We compare the difference in storage behavior between Na and Li in their analogous electrodes and summarize the sodium storage mechanisms in the available electrode materials.  This review also includes some new results from our group and our thoughts on developing new materials.  Some perspectives and directions on designing better materials for practical applications are pointed out based on knowledge from the literature and our experience.  Through this extensive literature review, the search for suitable electrode and electrolyte materials for stationary sodium-ion batteries is still challenging. In light of possible concerns over rising lithium costs in the future, Na and Na-ion batteries have re-emerged as candidates for medium and large-scale stationary energy storage, esp. as a result of heightened interest in renewable energy sources that provide intermittent power which needs to be load-levelled.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries have become a subject of increasing interest and are considered as an alternative to the ubiquitous lithium-ion battery.  To compare the effect of two improvement strategies for metal oxide cathodes, specifically Co-doping and morphol. optimization, four representatives of the prominent material class of layered NaxMO2 (M = transition metal) have been studied: hexagonal flakes and hollow spheres of P2-NaxMnO2 and P2-NaxCo0.1Mn0.9O2.  The better electrochem. performance of the spheres over the flakes and of the Co-doped over the undoped materials are explained on the basis of structural features revealed by operando synchrotron X-ray diffraction.  The higher cycling stability of the material doped with ∼10% Co is attributed to three effects: (i) the suppression of a Jahn-Teller-induced structural transition from the initial hexagonal to an orthorhombic phase that is obsd. in NaxMnO2, (ii) suppression of ordering processes of Na+; and (iii) enhanced Na+ kinetics as revealed by galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique measurements and in situ electrochem. impedance measurements.  Increased capacity and cycling stability of spheres over flakes may be related to smaller changes of the unit cell vol. of spheres and thus to reduced structural stress. The as-synthesized layered Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2 material exhibits a layered P2-type structure, and shows excellent electrochem. properties when studied as a cathode for Na-ion batteries, with a specific discharge capacity of 210 mAh g-1 and a good capacity retention of 80% at 50th cycle in the voltage range from 1.5 to 4.4 V at 0.1 C. The overall electrochem. performance of layered Na0.5Ni0.25Mn0.75O2 is quite promising, and hence the present study will greatly contribute to the on-going search for advanced and efficient electrodes for Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo6r0CFmAM2MLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhVgVqd8xt1Fg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XntVGitrY%253D&amp;md5=93bebc093ff70c4a2ca8872f36d4f54f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref8" class="rightTabRefNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit8" data-doi="10.1039/c5ra06275f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Facile Synthesis of P2-Type Na<sub>0.4</sub>Mn<sub>0.54</sub>Co<sub>0.46</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as a High Capacity Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>5</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">51454</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">51460</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rapid consumption of nonrenewable resources resulted in an ever-increasing problem of CO2 emissions that has motivated people for studying the harvesting of energy from renewable alternatives (e.g. solar and wind).  Efficient electrochem. energy storage devices play a crucial role in storing harvested energies in the daily lives.  For example, rechargeable batteries can store energy generated by solar cells during the daytime and release it during night-time.  In particular, Li-ion batteries (LIBs) have received considerable attention ever since their early commercialization in 1990s.  However, with initiatives by several governments to build large-scale energy grids to store energy for cities, problems such as the high cost and limited availability of Li starts to become major issues.  Na, which also belongs to Group 1 of the periodic table, has comparable electrochem. properties to Li, and more importantly it is considerably more accessible than Li.  Nonetheless, research into Na-ion batteries (NIBs) is currently still in its infancy compared to LIBs, although great leaps and bounds have been made recently in terms of research and development into this technol.  Here in this review, the authors summarize the recent advancements made, also covering the prospective materials for both the battery cathode and anode. This layered bronze possesses a hexagonal, P2-type structure, in which the distortion assocd. with Mn3+ is hardly noticeable.  It reacts slowly, though reversibly, with atm. moisture, which causes the interlayer spacing in the structure to increase by ∼2.5 Å, by intercalation of water mols. into the interlayer gap occupied by Na+ ions.  The anhyd. material was tested as a cathode in sodium cells.  Although the electrochem. intercalation of Na+ occurs in two steps, the host retains its main structural features, with a slight tendency in the interlayer spacing to contract as the sodium content increases.  The similarity between the discharge and charge profiles of the first cycles reveals a quasi-reversible nature of the intercalation process and that the cell can deliver a const. sp. capacity of ∼140 A-h/kg at 0.1 mA/cm2 when cycled in a voltage region of 3.8-2.0 V.  However, the continuous strains and distortions resulting from the insertion and extn. of Na+ ions cause the host structure to gradually collapse and yield an amorphous material after the first eight cycles.  This leads to a progressive redn. of the cell capacity, irresp. of the specific voltage region used.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpM1olI9SkShLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljZdhhPmOc0LA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD38Xit1KmsrY%253D&amp;md5=331badc7a20e6f46a269403f5d742182</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref14" class="rightTabRefNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit14" data-doi="10.1021/am406009t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bucher, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hartung, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nagasubramanian, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheah, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Due to its potential cost advantage, sodium ion batteries could become a com. alternative to lithium ion batteries.  One promising cathode material for this type of battery is layered sodium manganese oxide.  In this investigation the effect of morphol. on cycle performance for the layered NaxMnO2+z is reported.  Hollow spheres of NaxMnO2+z with a diam. of ∼5 μm were compared to flake-like NaxMnO2+z.  It was found that the electrochem. behavior of both materials as measured by cyclic voltammetry is comparable.  However, the cycle stability of the spheres is significantly higher, with 94 mA h g-1 discharge capacity after 100 cycles, as opposed to 73 mA h g-1 for the flakes (50 mA g-1).  The better stability can potentially be attributed to better accommodation of vol. changes of the material due to its spherical morphol., better contact with the added conductive carbon, and higher electrode/electrolyte interface owing to better wetting of the active material with the electrolyte.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpawRFnxN2xdrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgLdvJRM98x3w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXnvV2jsb8%253D&amp;md5=13ffb6207d1b6d23a2b8270439970f83</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref15" class="rightTabRefNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit15" data-doi="10.1039/c4ee03361b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ren, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishida, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Performance Symmetric Sodium-Ion Batteries Using a New, Bipolar O3-Type Material, Na<sub>0.8</sub>Ni<sub>0.4</sub>Ti<sub>0.6</sub>O<sub>2</sub></span>. <i>Energy Environ. However, there are some crit. drawbacks limiting its application, such as safety and stability problems.  In this work, a stable sym. sodium-ion battery based on the bipolar, active O3-type material, Na0.8Ni0.4Ti0.6O2, is developed.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Salts having a low m.p. are liq. at room temp., or even below, and form a new class of liqs. usually called room temp. ionic liqs. (RTIL).  Information about RTILs can be found in the literature with such key words as: room temp. molten salt, low-temp. molten salt, ambient-temp. molten salt, liq. org. salt or simply ionic liq.  Their physicochem. properties are the same as high temp. ionic liqs., but the practical aspects of their maintenance or handling are different enough to merit a distinction.  The class of ionic liqs., based on tetraalkylammonium cation and chloroaluminate anion, was extensively studied since late 1970s of the XX century, following the works of Osteryoung.  Systematic research on the application of chloroaluminate ionic liqs. as solvents was performed in 1980s.  However, ionic liqs. based on Al halides are moisture sensitive.  During the last decade an increasing no. of new ionic liqs. were prepd. and used as solvents.  The general aim of this paper was to review the phys. and chem. properties of RTILs from the point of view of their possible application as electrolytes in electrochem. processes and devices.  The following points are discussed: melting and freezing, cond., viscosity, temp. dependence of cond., transport and transference nos., electrochem. stability, possible application in Al electroplating, Li batteries and in electrochem. capacitors.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGo28DRQSuLWGLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgLdvJRM98x3w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XotVaqtr8%253D&amp;md5=09823d6515f7af16484295909b7b10e9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref19" class="rightTabRefNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit19" data-doi="10.1002/cphc.201402175"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, Y.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of recent progress in the development of several promising high-voltage (.ltorsim.5 V) electrolyte systems, as well as their "recipes" (for synthesis), electrochem. performance, electrode compatibility, and characterization methods.  These new electrolyte systems include high-voltage film-forming additives and new solvents, such as sulfones, ionic liqs., nitriles, and fluorinated carbonates.  It appears to be very difficult to find a good high-voltage (∼5 V) electrolyte with a single-component solvent at the present stage.  Other topics discussed include: (1) stabilizing the cathode-electrolyte interface with additives and surface coating, (2) new types of solvents for high-voltage electrolytes, (3) sulfone-based solvents, (4) ionic liq.-based systems, (5) nitrile co-solvents, and (6) derivs. of carbonate-based systems.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Energy prodn. and storage have become key issues concerning our welfare in daily life.  Present challenges for batteries are twofold.  In the first place, the increasing demand for powering systems of portable electronic devices and zero-emission vehicles stimulates research towards high energy and high voltage systems.  In the second place, low cost batteries are required in order to advance towards smart elec. grids that integrate discontinuous energy flow from renewable sources, optimizing the performance of clean energy sources.  Na-ion batteries can be the key for the second point, because of the huge availability of sodium, its low price and the similarity of both Li and Na insertion chemistries.  In spite of the lower energy d. and voltage of Na-ion based technologies, they can be focused on applications where the wt. and footprint requirement is less drastic, such as elec. grid storage.  Much work has to be done in the field of Na-ion in order to catch up with Li-ion technol.  Cathodic and anodic materials must be optimized, and new electrolytes will be the key point for Na-ion success.  This review will gather the up-to-date knowledge about Na-ion battery materials, with the aim of providing a wide view of the systems that have already been explored and a starting point for the new research on this battery technol.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoRYtxfVVW_sLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liCqKxZ_WpYpA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XivFWnu7s%253D&amp;md5=72869139086b8ee9adec746418ff84b1</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref27" class="rightTabRefNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit27" data-doi="10.1149/2.0091513jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Iermakova, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A comparative study of the electrode/electrolyte interface was carried out for lithium and sodium metal anodes in electrolytes consisting in 1 M LiPF6 in EC0.5:DMC0.5 (LP30) and 1 M NaPF6 in both EC0.5:DMC0.5 and EC0.45PC0.45DMC0.1. In contrast, large overpotentials were obsd. even at 0.1 mA/cm2 for Na/Na cells. A lower stability (partial soly.) of the SEI would also enable to understand the differences in the impedance of identical hard carbon (HC) electrodes in cells with either Li or Na counterelectrodes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A comprehensive study is reported entailing a comparison of Li, Na, K, Mg, and Ca based electrolytes and an investigation of the reliability of electrochem. tests using half-cells.  Ionic cond., viscosity, and Raman spectroscopy results point to the cation-solvent interaction to follow the polarizing power of the cations, i.e. Mg2+ &gt; Ca2+&gt; Li+ &gt; Na+ &gt; K+ and to divalent cation based electrolytes having stronger tendency to form ion pairs - lowering the cation accessibility and mobility.  Both increased temp. and the use of anions with delocalized neg. charge, such as TFSI, are effective in mitigating this issue.  Another factor impeding the divalent cations mobility is the larger solvation shells, as compared to those of monovalent cations, that in conjunction with stronger solvent - cation interactions contribute to slower charge transfer and ultimately a large impedance of Mg and Ca electrodes.  An important consequence is the non-reliability of the pseudo-ref. electrodes as these present both significant potential shifts as well as unstable behaviors.  Finally, exptl. protocols in order to achieve consistent results when using half-cell set-ups are proposed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoYVByTpUG3lrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgXK7mqOhkKIg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhtVCitL%252FM&amp;md5=b6e1096a51533a1e8108f4561f8cc48c</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1149/2.0981610jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dugas, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ponrouch, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gachot, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">David, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palacin, M. Different from lithium-oxygen cells, where lithium peroxide is found as the discharge product, sodium superoxide (NaO2) is formed in the present cell, with overpotentials as low as 100 mV during charging.  Several anal. methods are used to follow the cell reaction during discharge and charge.  Changes in structure and morphol. are studied by SEM and XRD.  It is found that NaO2 grows as cubic particles with feed sizes in the range of 10-50 μm; upon recharge the particles consecutively decomp.  Pressure monitoring during galvanostatic cycling shows that the coulombic efficiency (e-/O2) for discharge and charge is approx. 1.0, the expected value for NaO2 formation.  Also optical spectroscopy is identified as a convenient and useful tool to follow the discharge-charge process.  The max. discharge capacity is found to be limited by oxygen transport within the electrolyte soaked carbon fiber cathode and pore blocking near the oxygen interface is obsd.  Finally electrolyte decompn. and sodium dendrite growth are identified as possible reasons for the limited capacity retention of the cell.  The occurrence of undesired side reactions is analyzed by DEMS measurements during cycling as well as by post mortem XPS investigations.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGopL5ra_RP_GLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lik6BF_4bgKwg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtVartLfE&amp;md5=52469030077790d14ffd667d03bb0139</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1039/c5cp06184a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lahiri, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Borisenko, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Borodin, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Olschewski, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Endres, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Characterisation of the Solid Electrolyte Interface During Lithiation/Delithiation of Germanium in an Ionic Liquid</span>. <i>Phys.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Chem. reaction studies of N-methyl-N-propyl-pyrrolidinium-bis(fluorosulfonyl)imide-based ionic liq. with the Li metal surface were performed using ab initio mol. dynamics (aMD) simulations and XPS.  The mol. dynamics simulations showed rapid and spontaneous decompn. of the ionic liq. anion, with subsequent formation of long-lived species such as LiF.  The simulations also revealed the cation to retain its structure by generally moving away from the Li surface.  The XPS expts. showed evidence of decompn. of the anion, consistent with the aMD simulations and also of cation decompn. and it is envisaged that this is due to the longer time scale for the XPS expt. compared to the time scale of the aMD simulation.  Overall exptl. results confirm the majority of species suggested by the simulation. In the case of the Li surface, native surface species (e.g., Li2O, Li2CO3) persisted in the SEI and dominated the SEI compn.  The surface film formed on Li-deposited-on-Cu did not contain species assocd. with the Li native film.  Instead, in addn. to the anion redn. products, significant quantities of species assocd. with the cation were obsd.  EIS indicated varied Li conduction pathways through the film and that the pathways were in series, suggesting a layered structure. Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation. The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors analyzed the cathodic reactions of an important ionic liq. (IL) based electrolyte soln., Li bis(trifluoromethylsulfonyl)imide (LiTFSI)/N-butyl-N-methylpyrrolidinium (BMP) TFSI.  In situ FTIR spectroscopy was used for the anal. of gaseous products of the electrochem. decompn. of this IL soln. during cathodic polarization of Li metal and graphite electrodes.  The main volatile product of the reductive decompn. of the anion in these BMPTFSI solns. is trifluoromethane.  BMP cations decomp. to mixts. of tertiary amines and hydrocarbons.  The compn. of the products is influenced by the nature of the anode material.  Graphite possesses a catalytic activity in the electroredn. process of BMP cations which occurs along with their intercalation into the graphite structure.  The liq. phase after cathodic polarization of graphite electrodes was analyzed by multinuclear NMR spectroscopy coupled with FTIR spectroscopy. 15N NMR and FTIR spectra revealed an increase in the Li cations content in the electrolyte soln., of BMP cations decompn. during repeated cycling of graphite electrodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr84QZ2cpP9dbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljzS7xL6JEGyA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3cXitF2qsLw%253D&amp;md5=2e4bbe346561c8e3c9fe28e67187cb5a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1016/s0022-0728(02)01302-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Láng, G. G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Horányi, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Some Interesting Aspects of the Catalytic and Electrocatalytic Reduction of Perchlorate Ions</span>. <i>J. Electroanal. Chem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2003</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>552</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">197</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">211</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/s0022-0728(02)01302-5</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Some interesting aspects of the catalytic and electrocatalytic reduction of perchlorate ions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lang, G. G.; Horanyi, G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Electroanalytical Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2003</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">552</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">197-211</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JECHES</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Science B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A discussion.  The problem of the redn. of perchlorate ions is discussed in the light of recent results bearing in mind the perchlorate environmental contamination challenge and recent problems concerning the electrochem. stability of ClO4- ions.  The catalytic and electrocatalytic transformations are compared in the case of WC and Pt electrodes (catalysts).  Various methods for the detection of the occurrence of the redn. process are presented (radiotracer technique, potential step method and voltammetry).  The interpretation of the results of studies of corrosion processes of Al, Zn, Cu, Ni and Fe carried out in the presence of ClO4- ions cannot be considered as adequate without taking into consideration the redn. of ClO4- ions and all previous work should be revisited from the new findings.  Some elements of the mechanistic aspects of the complicated redn. processes are discussed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq5PErM-fqt_7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljzS7xL6JEGyA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD3sXmvF2mtbc%253D&amp;md5=c4a8e5e1f0d01521d0636fb7c6e3dd32</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1016/0022-0728(85)80090-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Colom, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">González-Tejera, M. J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Reduction of Perchlorate Ion on Ruthenium Electrodes in Aqueous Solutions</span>. <i>J. Electroanal. Chem. Interfacial Electrochem.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1985</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>190</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">243</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">255</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/0022-0728(85)80090-5</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Reduction of perchlorate ion on ruthenium electrodes in aqueous solutions</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Colom, F.; Gonzalez-Tejera, M. J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Electroanalytical Chemistry and Interfacial Electrochemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1985</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">190</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1-2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">243-55</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JEIEBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0022-0728</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">During slow potential scanning (&lt;10 mV/s) of the H region at electrodeposited Ru electrodes (Ru/Pt) in HClO4 solns. at room temp., a rapid change from anodic to cathodic current is recorded following the peak of oxidn. of adsorbed H.  The max. of this current reversal appears at potentials around 120 mV (vs. std. H electrode).  The neg. sweep of the voltammogram shows a current peak that corresponds to redn. of adsorbed O.  Addn. of H2SO4 or HCl to HClO4 solns. proves that the current reversal is due to the presence of ClO4-.  Anal. of the soln. after arresting the pos. sweep in the potential region of current reversal shows the formation of Cl-.  The redn. of ClO4- on Ru electrodes appears to be a very slow process which is only detected at low potential scan rates but is markedly enhanced by temp. and ClO4- concn.  The results suggest that ClO4- redn. proceeds through interaction of the anion and H adsorbed on the Ru electrode to the final products of Cl- and H2O.  The process seems to be related to the electrocatalytic activity of the electrode surface and the noble metal.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoFRDCvghQT47Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljzS7xL6JEGyA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL2MXltFaisr8%253D&amp;md5=930e7e356dd4b954d0940b2e10865ef7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1016/j.tetlet.2014.12.145"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Unzner, T. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Magauer, T.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Carbon–Fluorine Bond Activation for the Synthesis of Functionalized Molecules</span>. <i>Tetrahedron Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>56</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">877</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">883</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.tetlet.2014.12.145</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon-fluorine bond activation for the synthesis of functionalized molecules</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Unzner, Teresa A.; Magauer, Thomas</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Tetrahedron Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">56</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">877-883</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">TELEAY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0040-4039</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this review, we highlight recent progress in the activation of allylic and benzylic C-F bonds for the synthesis of functionalized mols.  The synthesis of fluorinated compds. as well as the use of these products as building blocks for the prepn. of complex mols. are fast growing research areas.  SN2' reactions of allylic difluoro and trifluoro compds. as well as transition-metal-catalyzed (nickel, palladium, platinum and copper) processes are described.  The C-F bond activation of 3-fluoropropenes was achieved with platinum- or organocatalysis.  Benzylic C-F bond activation was realized with magnesium by deprotonation leading to the formation of reactive quinone methide intermediates and with hydrogen bond donors by forming strong F···H interactions.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpQIOGxNd0KbLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljzS7xL6JEGyA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXptFGgtA%253D%253D&amp;md5=d48469e7aca69dc19cb5addd1adc884c</span></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i21">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.9b03279" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.9b03279</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Details of CV cycles of NaClO<sub>4</sub>-OR; NaFSI-IL; NaTFSI-IL; NaClO<sub>4</sub>-IL cells between 1.5 and 3.8 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> and 1.5–4.8 V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (NaFSI-IL); discharge capacity, Coulombic efficiency and charge/discharge profiles of cells; capacity retention of NaClO<sub>4</sub> 1 M-OR compared with NaClO<sub>4</sub> 0.5 M-OR, NaFSI 0.5 M-OR, and NaTFSI 0.5 M-OR; impedance spectra of NCO at the OCV state after cycling at 200 and 400 mA g<sup>–1</sup>; XPS survey spectrums and Cl 2p spectrums of electrodes after cycling; and SEM images of electrodes before and after cycling and fitting EIS results after 1 cycles, 30 cycles, and 500 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b03279/suppl_file/am9b03279_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b03279/suppl_file/am9b03279_si_001.pdf">am9b03279_si_001.pdf (1.68 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

	});

<script nonce="90da90e1ae43b325-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

)|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>
